<tr style="background:#f8eaba; border-top:2px solid transparent;">
<td colspan="2" style="text-align:center;"><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Chemical_infobox#References" title="Wikipedia:Chemical infobox">Infobox&#160;references</a></td>
</tr>
<tr style="background:#f8eaba;">
<td style="width:40%; max-height:1px; border-top:2px solid transparent; border-right:1px solid transparent;"></td>
<td style="width:60%; max-height:1px; border-top:2px solid transparent; border-left:1px solid transparent;"></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p><b>Carbon dioxide</b> (<a href="/wiki/Chemical_formula" title="Chemical formula">chemical formula</a> <b>CO<sub>2</sub></b>) is a colorless and odorless <a href="/wiki/Gas" title="Gas">gas</a> that is vital to <a href="/wiki/Life" title="Life">life on Earth</a>. This naturally occurring <a href="/wiki/Chemical_compound" title="Chemical compound">chemical compound</a> is made up of a <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">carbon</a> <a href="/wiki/Atom" title="Atom">atom</a> <a href="/wiki/Covalent_bond" title="Covalent bond">covalently</a> <a href="/wiki/Double_bond" title="Double bond">double bonded</a> to two <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a> atoms. Carbon dioxide exists in <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Earth" title="Atmosphere of Earth">Earth's atmosphere</a> as a <a href="/wiki/Trace_gas" title="Trace gas">trace gas</a> at a concentration of about 0.04 percent (400 <a href="/wiki/Parts_per_million" class="mw-redirect" title="Parts per million">ppm</a>) by volume.<sup id="cite_ref-NOAA_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NOAA-3">[3]</a></sup> Natural sources include <a href="/wiki/Volcano" title="Volcano">volcanoes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hot_spring" title="Hot spring">hot springs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Geyser" title="Geyser">geysers</a>, and it is freed from <a href="/wiki/Carbonate_rock" title="Carbonate rock">carbonate rocks</a> by <a href="/wiki/Dissolution_(chemistry)" title="Dissolution (chemistry)">dissolution</a> in water and acids. Because carbon dioxide is soluble in water, it occurs naturally in <a href="/wiki/Groundwater" title="Groundwater">groundwater</a>, <a href="/wiki/River" title="River">rivers</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lake" title="Lake">lakes</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Ice_cap" title="Ice cap">ice caps</a> and <a href="/wiki/Glacier" title="Glacier">glaciers</a> and also in <a href="/wiki/Seawater" title="Seawater">seawater</a>. It is present in deposits of <a href="/wiki/Petroleum" title="Petroleum">petroleum</a> and <a href="/wiki/Natural_gas" title="Natural gas">natural gas</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_carbon_dioxide" class="mw-redirect" title="Atmospheric carbon dioxide">Atmospheric carbon dioxide</a> is the primary source of carbon in life on Earth and its concentration in Earth's pre-industrial atmosphere since late in the <a href="/wiki/Precambrian" title="Precambrian">Precambrian</a> was regulated by <a href="/wiki/Photosynthesis" title="Photosynthesis">photosynthetic</a> organisms and geological phenomena. As part of the <a href="/wiki/Carbon_cycle" title="Carbon cycle">carbon cycle</a>, <a href="/wiki/Plant" title="Plant">plants</a>, <a href="/wiki/Algae" title="Algae">algae</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Cyanobacteria" title="Cyanobacteria">cyanobacteria</a> use <a href="/wiki/Light" title="Light">light</a> <a href="/wiki/Energy" title="Energy">energy</a> to <a href="/wiki/Photosynthesis" title="Photosynthesis">photosynthesize</a> <a href="/wiki/Carbohydrate" title="Carbohydrate">carbohydrate</a> from carbon dioxide and water, with oxygen produced as a waste product.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup></p>
<p>Carbon dioxide (CO<sub>2</sub>) is produced by all <a href="/wiki/Aerobic_organisms" class="mw-redirect" title="Aerobic organisms">aerobic organisms</a> when they metabolize carbohydrates and lipids to produce energy by <a href="/wiki/Cellular_respiration" title="Cellular respiration">respiration</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> It is returned to water via the <a href="/wiki/Fish_respiration" class="mw-redirect" title="Fish respiration">gills of fish</a> and to the air via the lungs of air-breathing land animals, including humans. Carbon dioxide is produced during the processes of <a href="/wiki/Decomposition" title="Decomposition">decay</a> of organic materials and the <a href="/wiki/Fermentation" title="Fermentation">fermentation</a> of sugars in <a href="/wiki/Bread" title="Bread">bread</a>, <a href="/wiki/Beer" title="Beer">beer</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wine" title="Wine">winemaking</a>. It is produced by combustion of <a href="/wiki/Wood" title="Wood">wood</a> and other organic materials and <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuel" title="Fossil fuel">fossil fuels</a> such as coal, <a href="/wiki/Peat" title="Peat">peat</a>, <a href="/wiki/Petroleum" title="Petroleum">petroleum</a> and <a href="/wiki/Natural_gas" title="Natural gas">natural gas</a>.</p>
<p>It is a versatile industrial material, used, for example, as an inert gas in welding and fire extinguishers, as a pressurizing gas in air guns and oil recovery, as a chemical feedstock and in liquid form as a solvent in decaffeination of coffee and <a href="/wiki/Supercritical_drying" title="Supercritical drying">supercritical drying</a>. It is added to drinking water and <a href="/wiki/Carbonated_beverage" class="mw-redirect" title="Carbonated beverage">carbonated beverages</a> including <a href="/wiki/Beer" title="Beer">beer</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sparkling_wine" title="Sparkling wine">sparkling wine</a> to add <a href="/wiki/Effervescence" title="Effervescence">effervescence</a>. The frozen solid form of CO<sub>2</sub>, known as "<a href="/wiki/Dry_ice" title="Dry ice">dry ice</a>" is used as a refrigerant and as an abrasive in <a href="/wiki/Dry-ice_blasting" title="Dry-ice blasting">dry-ice blasting</a>.</p>
<p>Carbon dioxide is the most significant long-lived <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas" title="Greenhouse gas">greenhouse gas</a> in <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide_in_Earth%27s_atmosphere" title="Carbon dioxide in Earth's atmosphere">Earth's atmosphere</a>. Since the <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">Industrial Revolution</a> anthropogenic emissions - primarily from use of fossil fuels and <a href="/wiki/Deforestation" title="Deforestation">deforestation</a> - have rapidly increased its concentration in the atmosphere, leading to <a href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">global warming</a>. Carbon dioxide also causes <a href="/wiki/Ocean_acidification" title="Ocean acidification">ocean acidification</a> because it dissolves in water to form <a href="/wiki/Carbonic_acid" title="Carbonic acid">carbonic acid</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-NRC2010_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NRC2010-7">[7]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<ul>
<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-1"><a href="#Background"><span class="tocnumber">1</span> <span class="toctext">Background</span></a></li>
<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-2"><a href="#Chemical_and_physical_properties"><span class="tocnumber">2</span> <span class="toctext">Chemical and physical properties</span></a>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Background">Background</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_dioxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Background">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Carbon-dioxide-crystal-3D-vdW.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Carbon-dioxide-crystal-3D-vdW.png/170px-Carbon-dioxide-crystal-3D-vdW.png" width="170" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Carbon-dioxide-crystal-3D-vdW.png/255px-Carbon-dioxide-crystal-3D-vdW.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Carbon-dioxide-crystal-3D-vdW.png/340px-Carbon-dioxide-crystal-3D-vdW.png 2x" data-file-width="1100" data-file-height="1066" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Carbon-dioxide-crystal-3D-vdW.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Crystal structure of <a href="/wiki/Dry_ice" title="Dry ice">dry ice</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Carbon dioxide was the first gas to be described as a discrete substance. In about 1640,<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Flemish_people" title="Flemish people">Flemish</a> chemist <a href="/wiki/Jan_Baptist_van_Helmont" title="Jan Baptist van Helmont">Jan Baptist van Helmont</a> observed that when he burned <a href="/wiki/Charcoal" title="Charcoal">charcoal</a> in a closed vessel, the mass of the resulting <a href="/wiki/Ash_(analytical_chemistry)" title="Ash (analytical chemistry)">ash</a> was much less than that of the original charcoal. His interpretation was that the rest of the charcoal had been transmuted into an invisible substance he termed a "gas" or "wild spirit" (<i>spiritus sylvestre</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup></p>
<p>The properties of carbon dioxide were studied more thoroughly in the 1750s by the <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scottish</a> physician <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Black" title="Joseph Black">Joseph Black</a>. He found that <a href="/wiki/Limestone" title="Limestone">limestone</a> (<a href="/wiki/Calcium_carbonate" title="Calcium carbonate">calcium carbonate</a>) could be heated or treated with <a href="/wiki/Acid" title="Acid">acids</a> to yield a gas he called "fixed air." He observed that the fixed air was denser than air and supported neither flame nor animal life. Black also found that when bubbled through <a href="/wiki/Limewater" title="Limewater">limewater</a> (a saturated aqueous solution of <a href="/wiki/Calcium_hydroxide" title="Calcium hydroxide">calcium hydroxide</a>), it would <a href="/wiki/Precipitation_(chemistry)" title="Precipitation (chemistry)">precipitate</a> calcium carbonate. He used this phenomenon to illustrate that carbon dioxide is produced by animal respiration and microbial fermentation. In 1772, English chemist <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Priestley" title="Joseph Priestley">Joseph Priestley</a> published a paper entitled <i>Impregnating Water with Fixed Air</i> in which he described a process of dripping <a href="/wiki/Sulfuric_acid" title="Sulfuric acid">sulfuric acid</a> (or <i>oil of vitriol</i> as Priestley knew it) on chalk in order to produce carbon dioxide, and forcing the gas to dissolve by agitating a bowl of water in contact with the gas.<sup id="cite_ref-Priestley_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Priestley-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
<p>Carbon dioxide was first liquefied (at elevated pressures) in 1823 by <a href="/wiki/Humphry_Davy" title="Humphry Davy">Humphry Davy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Michael_Faraday" title="Michael Faraday">Michael Faraday</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Davy_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Davy-11">[11]</a></sup> The earliest description of solid carbon dioxide was given by <a href="/wiki/Adrien-Jean-Pierre_Thilorier" title="Adrien-Jean-Pierre Thilorier">Adrien-Jean-Pierre Thilorier</a>, who in 1835 opened a pressurized container of liquid carbon dioxide, only to find that the cooling produced by the rapid evaporation of the liquid yielded a "snow" of solid CO<sub>2</sub>.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Chemical_and_physical_properties">Chemical and physical properties</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_dioxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Chemical and physical properties">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Structure_and_bonding">Structure and bonding</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_dioxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Structure and bonding">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Molecular_orbital_diagram#Carbon_dioxide" title="Molecular orbital diagram">Molecular orbital diagram §&#160;Carbon dioxide</a></div>
<div class="floatleft"><a href="/wiki/File:Co2comp.png" class="image"><img alt="Co2comp.png" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Co2comp.png/250px-Co2comp.png" width="250" height="78" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Co2comp.png/375px-Co2comp.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Co2comp.png/500px-Co2comp.png 2x" data-file-width="1507" data-file-height="468" /></a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Co2_vibrations.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Co2_vibrations.svg/220px-Co2_vibrations.svg.png" width="220" height="79" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Co2_vibrations.svg/330px-Co2_vibrations.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Co2_vibrations.svg/440px-Co2_vibrations.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="886" data-file-height="319" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Co2_vibrations.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Infrared_spectroscopy#Number_of_vibrational_modes" title="Infrared spectroscopy">Stretching and bending oscillations</a> of the CO<sub>2</sub> carbon dioxide molecule. Upper left: symmetric stretching. Upper right: antisymmetric stretching. Lower line: degenerate pair of bending modes.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The carbon dioxide molecule is linear and <a href="/wiki/Centrosymmetric" class="mw-redirect" title="Centrosymmetric">centrosymmetric</a>. The two C=O bonds are equivalent and are short (116.3 <a href="/wiki/Picometer" class="mw-redirect" title="Picometer">pm</a>), consistent with double bonding.<sup id="cite_ref-Green_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Green-14">[14]</a></sup> Since it is centrosymmetric, the molecule has no electrical <a href="/wiki/Dipole" title="Dipole">dipole</a>. Consequently, only two vibrational bands are observed in the <a href="/wiki/IR_spectrum" class="mw-redirect" title="IR spectrum">IR spectrum</a> – an antisymmetric stretching mode at 2349&#160;cm<sup>−1</sup> and a <a href="/wiki/Degenerate_energy_levels" title="Degenerate energy levels">degenerate</a> pair of bending modes at 667&#160;cm<sup>−1</sup>. There is also a symmetric stretching mode at 1388&#160;cm<sup>−1</sup> which is only observed in the <a href="/wiki/Raman_spectrum" class="mw-redirect" title="Raman spectrum">Raman spectrum</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_aqueous_solution">In aqueous solution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_dioxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: In aqueous solution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Carbonic_acid" title="Carbonic acid">Carbonic acid</a></div>
<p>Carbon dioxide is <a href="/wiki/Soluble" class="mw-redirect" title="Soluble">soluble</a> in water, in which it reversibly forms <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
2</span>CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
3</span></span> (<a href="/wiki/Carbonic_acid" title="Carbonic acid">carbonic acid</a>), which is a <a href="/wiki/Acid_strength" title="Acid strength">weak acid</a> since its ionization in water is incomplete.</p>
<dl>
<dd><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
2</span></span> + <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
2</span>O</span> ⇌ <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
2</span>CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
3</span></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Henry%27s_law" title="Henry's law">hydration equilibrium constant</a> of carbonic acid is <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" >
  <semantics>
    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
      <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
        <msub>
          <mi>K</mi>
          <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
            <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
              <mi mathvariant="normal">h</mi>
            </mrow>
          </mrow>
        </msub>
        <mo>=</mo>
        <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
          <mfrac>
            <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

                <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                  <mn>2</mn>
                </mrow>
              </msub>
              <mi mathvariant="normal">C</mi>
              <msub>
                <mi mathvariant="normal">O</mi>
                <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                  <mn>3</mn>
                </mrow>
              </msub>
              <mo stretchy="false">]</mo>
            </mrow>
            <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
              <mo stretchy="false">[</mo>
              <mi mathvariant="normal">C</mi>
              <msub>
                <mi mathvariant="normal">O</mi>
                <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                  <mn>2</mn>
                </mrow>
              </msub>

        <mo>&#x00D7;<!-- × --></mo>

          <mn>10</mn>

            <mo>&#x2212;<!-- − --></mo>
            <mn>3</mn>
          </mrow>
        </msup>
      </mstyle>
    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle K_{\mathrm {h} }={\frac {\rm {[H_{2}CO_{3}]}}{\rm {[CO_{2}(aq)]}}}=1.70\times 10^{-3}}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/880be9280cefa51b14dc80f037e4296c81a15e6b" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -2.671ex; width:32.052ex; height:6.509ex;" alt="K_{\mathrm {h} }={\frac {\rm {[H_{2}CO_{3}]}}{\rm {[CO_{2}(aq)]}}}=1.70\times 10^{-3}" /></span> (at 25&#160;°C). Hence, the majority of the carbon dioxide is not converted into carbonic acid, but remains as CO<sub>2</sub> molecules, not affecting the pH.</p>
<p>The relative concentrations of <span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
2</span>, H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
2</span>CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
3</span></span>, and the <a href="/wiki/Deprotonation" title="Deprotonation">deprotonated</a> forms <span class="chemf nowrap">HCO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">−<br />
3</span></span> (<a href="/wiki/Bicarbonate" title="Bicarbonate">bicarbonate</a>) and <span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">2−<br />
3</span></span>(<a href="/wiki/Carbonate" title="Carbonate">carbonate</a>) depend on the <a href="/wiki/PH" title="PH">pH</a>. As shown in a <a href="/wiki/Bjerrum_plot" title="Bjerrum plot">Bjerrum plot</a>, in neutral or slightly alkaline water (pH &gt; 6.5), the bicarbonate form predominates (&gt;50%) becoming the most prevalent (&gt;95%) at the pH of seawater. In very alkaline water (pH &gt; 10.4), the predominant (&gt;50%) form is carbonate. The oceans, being mildly alkaline with typical pH = 8.2–8.5, contain about 120&#160;mg of bicarbonate per liter.</p>
<p>Being <a href="/wiki/Diprotic_acid" title="Diprotic acid">diprotic</a>, carbonic acid has two <a href="/wiki/Acid_dissociation_constant" title="Acid dissociation constant">acid dissociation constants</a>, the first one for the dissociation into the bicarbonate (also called hydrogen carbonate) ion (HCO<sub>3</sub><sup>−</sup>):</p>
<dl>
<dd>H<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> ⇌ HCO<sub>3</sub><sup>−</sup> + H<sup>+</sup></dd>
<dd><i>K</i><sub>a1</sub> = <span class="nowrap"><span style="display:none" class="sortkey">6999250000000000000♠</span>2.5<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">×</span>10<sup>−4</sup>&#160;mol/L</span>; p<i>K</i><sub>a1</sub> = 3.6 at 25 °C.<sup id="cite_ref-Green_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Green-14">[14]</a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<p>This is the <i>true</i> first acid dissociation constant, defined as <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" >
  <semantics>
    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
      <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
        <msub>
          <mi>K</mi>
          <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
            <mi>a</mi>
            <mn>1</mn>
          </mrow>
        </msub>
        <mo>=</mo>
        <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
          <mfrac>
            <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
              <mo stretchy="false">[</mo>
              <mi mathvariant="normal">H</mi>
              <mi mathvariant="normal">C</mi>
              <msubsup>
                <mi mathvariant="normal">O</mi>
                <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                  <mn>3</mn>
                </mrow>
                <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                  <mo>&#x2212;<!-- − --></mo>
                </mrow>
              </msubsup>
              <mo stretchy="false">]</mo>
              <mo stretchy="false">[</mo>

                <mi mathvariant="normal">H</mi>
                <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                  <mo>+</mo>
                </mrow>
              </msup>
              <mo stretchy="false">]</mo>
            </mrow>

                <mi mathvariant="normal">H</mi>
                <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                  <mn>2</mn>
                </mrow>
              </msub>
              <mi mathvariant="normal">C</mi>
              <msub>
                <mi mathvariant="normal">O</mi>
                <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                  <mn>3</mn>
                </mrow>
              </msub>
              <mo stretchy="false">]</mo>
            </mrow>
          </mfrac>
        </mrow>
      </mstyle>
    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle K_{a1}={\frac {\rm {[HCO_{3}^{-}][H^{+}]}}{\rm {[H_{2}CO_{3}]}}}}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/81950791f8b36374241d6fa82828522bd875668a" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -2.671ex; width:20.547ex; height:6.676ex;" alt="K_{a1}={\frac {\rm {[HCO_{3}^{-}][H^{+}]}}{\rm {[H_{2}CO_{3}]}}}" /></span>, where the denominator includes only covalently bound H<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> and does not include hydrated CO<sub>2</sub>(aq). The much smaller and often-quoted value near <span class="nowrap"><span style="display:none" class="sortkey">6993416000000000000♠</span>4.16<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">×</span>10<sup>−7</sup></span> is an <i>apparent</i> value calculated on the (incorrect) assumption that all dissolved CO<sub>2</sub> is present as carbonic acid, so that <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" >
  <semantics>
    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

              <mo stretchy="false">]</mo>
              <mo>+</mo>
              <mo stretchy="false">[</mo>

                <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                  <mn>2</mn>
                </mrow>

    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle K_{\mathrm {a1} }{\rm {(apparent)}}={\frac {\rm {[HCO_{3}^{-}][H^{+}]}}{\rm {[H_{2}CO_{3}]+[CO_{2}(aq)]}}}}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/d4cac9482fd21cb58444f2621f245d7b1d835485" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -2.671ex; width:40.474ex; height:6.676ex;" alt="K_{\mathrm {a1} }{\rm {(apparent)}}={\frac {\rm {[HCO_{3}^{-}][H^{+}]}}{\rm {[H_{2}CO_{3}]+[CO_{2}(aq)]}}}" /></span>. Since most of the dissolved CO<sub>2</sub> remains as CO<sub>2</sub> molecules, <i>K</i><sub>a1</sub>(apparent) has a much larger denominator and a much smaller value than the true <i>K</i><sub>a1</sub>.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Bicarbonate" title="Bicarbonate">bicarbonate</a> ion is an <a href="/wiki/Amphoteric" class="mw-redirect" title="Amphoteric">amphoteric</a> species that can act as an acid or as a base, depending on pH of the solution. At high <a href="/wiki/PH" title="PH">pH</a>, it dissociates significantly into the <a href="/wiki/Carbonate" title="Carbonate">carbonate</a> ion (CO<sub>3</sub><sup>2−</sup>):</p>
<dl>
<dd>HCO<sub>3</sub><sup>−</sup> ⇌ CO<sub>3</sub><sup>2−</sup> + H<sup>+</sup></dd>
<dd><i>K</i><sub>a2</sub> = <span class="nowrap"><span style="display:none" class="sortkey">6992469000000000000♠</span>4.69<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">×</span>10<sup>−11</sup>&#160;mol/L</span>; p<i>K</i><sub>a2</sub> = 10.329</dd>
</dl>
<p>In organisms carbonic acid production is catalysed by the <a href="/wiki/Enzyme" title="Enzyme">enzyme</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carbonic_anhydrase" title="Carbonic anhydrase">carbonic anhydrase</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Chemical_reactions_of_CO2">Chemical reactions of CO<sub>2</sub></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_dioxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Chemical reactions of CO2">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox mbox-small-left ambox-content" role="presentation">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image"><a href="/wiki/File:Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg" class="image"><img alt="[icon]" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/20px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png" width="20" height="14" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/30px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/40px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="44" data-file-height="31" /></a></td>
<td class="mbox-text"><span class="mbox-text-span"><b>This section needs expansion</b>. <small>You can help by <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Carbon_dioxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=">adding to it</a>.</small> <small><i>(June 2014)</i></small></span></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>CO<sub>2</sub> is a weak <a href="/wiki/Electrophile" title="Electrophile">electrophile</a>. Its reaction with basic water illustrates this property, in which case <a href="/wiki/Hydroxide" title="Hydroxide">hydroxide</a> is the <a href="/wiki/Nucleophile" title="Nucleophile">nucleophile</a>. Other nucleophiles react as well. For example, <a href="/wiki/Carbanion" title="Carbanion">carbanions</a> as provided by <a href="/wiki/Grignard_reagent" class="mw-redirect" title="Grignard reagent">Grignard reagents</a> and <a href="/wiki/Organolithium_compound" class="mw-redirect" title="Organolithium compound">organolithium compounds</a> react with CO<sub>2</sub> to give carboxylates:</p>
<dl>
<dd>MR + CO<sub>2</sub> → RCO<sub>2</sub>M</dd>
<dd>where M = Li or MgBr and R = <a href="/wiki/Alkyl" title="Alkyl">alkyl</a> or <a href="/wiki/Aryl" title="Aryl">aryl</a>.</dd>
</dl>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Metal_carbon_dioxide_complex" title="Metal carbon dioxide complex">metal carbon dioxide complexes</a>, CO<sub>2</sub> serves as a ligand, which can facilitate the conversion of CO<sub>2</sub> to other chemicals.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup></p>
<p>The reduction of CO<sub>2</sub> to CO is ordinarily a difficult and slow reaction:</p>
<dl>
<dd>CO<sub>2</sub> + 2 e<sup>−</sup> + 2H<sup>+</sup> → CO + H<sub>2</sub>O</dd>
</dl>
<p>The redox potential for this reaction near pH 7 is about −0.53 V <i>versus</i> the <a href="/wiki/Standard_hydrogen_electrode" title="Standard hydrogen electrode">standard hydrogen electrode</a>. The nickel-containing enzyme <a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide_dehydrogenase" title="Carbon monoxide dehydrogenase">carbon monoxide dehydrogenase</a> catalyses this process.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Physical_properties">Physical properties</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_dioxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Physical properties">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">For more details on this topic, see <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide_data" class="mw-redirect" title="Carbon dioxide data">Carbon dioxide data</a>.</div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Carbon_dioxide_pressure-temperature_phase_diagram.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Carbon_dioxide_pressure-temperature_phase_diagram.svg/220px-Carbon_dioxide_pressure-temperature_phase_diagram.svg.png" width="220" height="208" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Carbon_dioxide_pressure-temperature_phase_diagram.svg/330px-Carbon_dioxide_pressure-temperature_phase_diagram.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Carbon_dioxide_pressure-temperature_phase_diagram.svg/440px-Carbon_dioxide_pressure-temperature_phase_diagram.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="742" data-file-height="700" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Carbon_dioxide_pressure-temperature_phase_diagram.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Carbon dioxide pressure-temperature phase diagram showing the <a href="/wiki/Triple_point" title="Triple point">triple point</a> and <a href="/wiki/Critical_point_(thermodynamics)" title="Critical point (thermodynamics)">critical point</a> of carbon dioxide</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Dry_Ice_Pellets_Subliming.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Dry_Ice_Pellets_Subliming.jpg/220px-Dry_Ice_Pellets_Subliming.jpg" width="220" height="189" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Dry_Ice_Pellets_Subliming.jpg/330px-Dry_Ice_Pellets_Subliming.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Dry_Ice_Pellets_Subliming.jpg/440px-Dry_Ice_Pellets_Subliming.jpg 2x" data-file-width="609" data-file-height="523" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Dry_Ice_Pellets_Subliming.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Sample of solid carbon dioxide or "dry ice" pellets</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Carbon dioxide is colorless. At low concentrations, the gas is odorless. At higher concentrations it has a sharp, acidic odor. At <a href="/wiki/Standard_conditions_for_temperature_and_pressure" title="Standard conditions for temperature and pressure">standard temperature and pressure</a>, the density of carbon dioxide is around 1.98&#160;kg/m<sup>3</sup>, about 1.67 times that of <a href="/wiki/Earth%27s_atmosphere" class="mw-redirect" title="Earth's atmosphere">air</a>.</p>
<p>Carbon dioxide has no liquid state at pressures below 5.1 <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_(unit)" title="Atmosphere (unit)">standard atmospheres</a> (520&#160;kPa). At 1 atmosphere (near mean sea level pressure), the gas <a href="/wiki/Deposition_(physics)" class="mw-redirect" title="Deposition (physics)">deposits</a> directly to a solid at temperatures below −78.5&#160;°C (−109.3&#160;°F; 194.7&#160;K) and the solid <a href="/wiki/Sublimation_(chemistry)" class="mw-redirect" title="Sublimation (chemistry)">sublimes</a> directly to a gas above −78.5&#160;°C. In its solid state, carbon dioxide is commonly called <a href="/wiki/Dry_ice" title="Dry ice">dry ice</a>.</p>
<p>Liquid carbon dioxide forms only at <a href="/wiki/Pressure" title="Pressure">pressures</a> above 5.1 atm; the <a href="/wiki/Triple_point" title="Triple point">triple point</a> of carbon dioxide is about 518 <a href="/wiki/KPa" class="mw-redirect" title="KPa">kPa</a> at −56.6&#160;°C (see phase diagram at left). The <a href="/wiki/Critical_point_(thermodynamics)" title="Critical point (thermodynamics)">critical point</a> is 7.38 MPa at 31.1&#160;°C.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> Another form of solid carbon dioxide observed at high pressure is an <a href="/wiki/Amorphous" class="mw-redirect" title="Amorphous">amorphous</a> glass-like solid.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> This form of glass, called <i><a href="/wiki/Amorphous_carbonia" title="Amorphous carbonia">carbonia</a></i>, is produced by <a href="/wiki/Supercooling" title="Supercooling">supercooling</a> heated CO<sub>2</sub> at extreme pressure (40–48 <a href="/wiki/GPa" class="mw-redirect" title="GPa">GPa</a> or about 400,000 atmospheres) in a <a href="/wiki/Diamond_anvil" class="mw-redirect" title="Diamond anvil">diamond anvil</a>. This discovery confirmed the theory that carbon dioxide could exist in a glass state similar to other members of its elemental family, like <a href="/wiki/Silicon" title="Silicon">silicon</a> (<a href="/wiki/Silica" class="mw-redirect" title="Silica">silica glass</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Germanium_dioxide" title="Germanium dioxide">germanium dioxide</a>. Unlike silica and germania glasses, however, carbonia glass is not stable at normal pressures and reverts to gas when pressure is released.</p>
<p>At temperatures and pressures above the critical point, carbon dioxide behaves as a <a href="/wiki/Supercritical_fluid" title="Supercritical fluid">supercritical fluid</a> known as <a href="/wiki/Supercritical_carbon_dioxide" title="Supercritical carbon dioxide">supercritical carbon dioxide</a>.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Isolation_and_production">Isolation and production</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_dioxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Isolation and production">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Carbon dioxide can be obtained by <a href="/wiki/Distillation" title="Distillation">distillation</a> from air, but the method is inefficient. Industrially, carbon dioxide is predominantly an unrecovered waste product, produced by several methods which may be practiced at various scales.<sup id="cite_ref-kirk_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kirk-22">[22]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Combustion" title="Combustion">combustion</a> of all <a href="/wiki/Carbon-based_fuel" title="Carbon-based fuel">carbon-based fuels</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a> (<a href="/wiki/Natural_gas" title="Natural gas">natural gas</a>), petroleum distillates (<a href="/wiki/Gasoline" title="Gasoline">gasoline</a>, <a href="/wiki/Diesel_fuel" title="Diesel fuel">diesel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kerosene" title="Kerosene">kerosene</a>, <a href="/wiki/Propane" title="Propane">propane</a>), coal, wood and generic organic matter produces carbon dioxide and, except in the case of pure carbon, water. As an example, the chemical reaction between methane and oxygen:</p>
<dl>
<dd><span class="chemf nowrap">CH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
4</span>+ 2 O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
2</span>→ CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
2</span>+ 2 H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
2</span>O</span></dd>
</dl>
<p>It is produced by thermal decomposition of limestone, <span class="chemf nowrap">CaCO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
3</span></span> by heating (<a href="/wiki/Calcining" class="mw-redirect" title="Calcining">calcining</a>) at about 850&#160;°C (1,560&#160;°F), in the manufacture of <a href="/wiki/Calcium_oxide" title="Calcium oxide">quicklime</a> (<a href="/wiki/Calcium_oxide" title="Calcium oxide">calcium oxide</a>, <span class="chemf nowrap">CaO</span>), a compound that has many industrial uses:</p>
<dl>
<dd><span class="chemf nowrap">CaCO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
3</span>→ CaO + CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
2</span></span></dd>
</dl>
<p><a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">Iron</a> is reduced from its oxides with <a href="/wiki/Coke_(fuel)" title="Coke (fuel)">coke</a> in a <a href="/wiki/Blast_furnace" title="Blast furnace">blast furnace</a>, producing <a href="/wiki/Pig_iron" title="Pig iron">pig iron</a> and carbon dioxide:<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup></p>
<p>Carbon dioxide is a byproduct of the industrial production of hydrogen by <a href="/wiki/Steam_reforming" title="Steam reforming">steam reforming</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ammonia_production" title="Ammonia production">ammonia synthesis</a>. These processes begin with the reaction of water and natural gas (mainly methane).<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup></p>
<p>Acids liberate <span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
2</span></span> from most metal carbonates. Consequently, it may be obtained directly from natural carbon dioxide <a href="/wiki/Spring_(hydrosphere)" class="mw-redirect" title="Spring (hydrosphere)">springs</a>, where it is produced by the action of acidified water on <a href="/wiki/Limestone" title="Limestone">limestone</a> or <a href="/wiki/Dolomite" title="Dolomite">dolomite</a>. The reaction between <a href="/wiki/Hydrochloric_acid" title="Hydrochloric acid">hydrochloric acid</a> and calcium carbonate (limestone or chalk) is shown below:</p>
<dl>
<dd><span class="chemf nowrap">CaCO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
3</span>+ 2 HCl → CaCl<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
2</span>+ H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
2</span>CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
3</span></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Carbonic_acid" title="Carbonic acid">carbonic acid</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
2</span>CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
3</span></span>) then decomposes to water and <span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
2</span></span>:</p>
<dl>
<dd><span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
2</span>CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
3</span>→ CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
2</span>+ H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
2</span>O</span></dd>
</dl>
<p>Such reactions are accompanied by foaming or bubbling, or both, as the gas is released. They have widespread uses in industry because they can be used to neutralize waste acid streams.</p>
<p>Carbon dioxide is a by-product of the <a href="/wiki/Fermentation_(biochemistry)" class="mw-redirect" title="Fermentation (biochemistry)">fermentation</a> of <a href="/wiki/Sugar" title="Sugar">sugar</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Brewing" title="Brewing">brewing</a> of <a href="/wiki/Beer" title="Beer">beer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Whisky" title="Whisky">whisky</a> and other <a href="/wiki/Alcoholic_beverage" class="mw-redirect" title="Alcoholic beverage">alcoholic beverages</a> and in the production of <a href="/wiki/Bioethanol" class="mw-redirect" title="Bioethanol">bioethanol</a>. <a href="/wiki/Yeast" title="Yeast">Yeast</a> metabolizes <a href="/wiki/Sugar" title="Sugar">sugar</a> to produce <span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
2</span></span> and <a href="/wiki/Ethanol" title="Ethanol">ethanol</a>, also known as alcohol, as follows:</p>
<dl>
<dd><span class="chemf nowrap">C<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
6</span>H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
12</span>O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
6</span></span> → <span class="chemf nowrap">2 CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
2</span>+ 2 C<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
2</span>H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
5</span>OH</span></dd>
</dl>
<p>All <a href="/wiki/Cellular_respiration" title="Cellular respiration">aerobic</a> organisms produce <span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
2</span></span> when they oxidize <a href="/wiki/Carbohydrate" title="Carbohydrate">carbohydrates</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fatty_acid" title="Fatty acid">fatty acids</a>, and proteins. The large number of reactions involved are exceedingly complex and not described easily. Refer to (<a href="/wiki/Cellular_respiration" title="Cellular respiration">cellular respiration</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anaerobic_respiration" title="Anaerobic respiration">anaerobic respiration</a> and <a href="/wiki/Photosynthesis" title="Photosynthesis">photosynthesis</a>). The equation for the respiration of glucose and other <a href="/wiki/Monosaccharide" title="Monosaccharide">monosaccharides</a> is:</p>
<dl>
<dd><span class="chemf nowrap">C<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
6</span>H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
12</span>O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
6</span></span> + <span class="chemf nowrap">6 O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
2</span></span> → <span class="chemf nowrap">6 CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
2</span></span> + <span class="chemf nowrap">6 H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
2</span>O</span></dd>
</dl>
<p><a href="/wiki/Photoautotrophs" class="mw-redirect" title="Photoautotrophs">Photoautotrophs</a> (i.e. plants and <a href="/wiki/Cyanobacteria" title="Cyanobacteria">cyanobacteria</a>) use the energy contained in sunlight to <a href="/wiki/Photosynthesis" title="Photosynthesis">photosynthesize</a> simple sugars from <span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
2</span></span> absorbed from the air and water:</p>
<dl>
<dd><i>n</i> <span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
2</span></span> + <i>n</i> <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
2</span>O</span> → <span class="chemf nowrap">(CH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
2</span>O)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
<i>n</i></span></span> + <i>n</i> <span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
2</span></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>Carbon dioxide comprises about 40-45% of the gas that emanates from decomposition in landfills (termed "landfill gas"). Most of the remaining 50-55% is methane.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Applications">Applications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_dioxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Applications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Soda_bubbles_macro.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Soda_bubbles_macro.jpg/250px-Soda_bubbles_macro.jpg" width="250" height="167" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Soda_bubbles_macro.jpg/375px-Soda_bubbles_macro.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Soda_bubbles_macro.jpg/500px-Soda_bubbles_macro.jpg 2x" data-file-width="750" data-file-height="500" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Soda_bubbles_macro.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Carbon dioxide bubbles in a soft drink.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Carbon dioxide is used by the food industry, the oil industry, and the chemical industry.<sup id="cite_ref-kirk_22-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kirk-22">[22]</a></sup> The compound has varied commercial uses but one of its greatest use as a chemical is in the production of carbonated beverages; it provides the sparkle in carbonated beverages such as soda water.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Precursor_to_chemicals">Precursor to chemicals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_dioxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Precursor to chemicals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox mbox-small-left ambox-content" role="presentation">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image"><a href="/wiki/File:Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg" class="image"><img alt="[icon]" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/20px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png" width="20" height="14" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/30px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/40px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="44" data-file-height="31" /></a></td>
<td class="mbox-text"><span class="mbox-text-span"><b>This section needs expansion</b>. <small>You can help by <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Carbon_dioxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=">adding to it</a>.</small> <small><i>(July 2014)</i></small></span></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>In the chemical industry, carbon dioxide is mainly consumed as an ingredient in the production of <a href="/wiki/Urea" title="Urea">urea</a>, with a smaller fraction being used to produce <a href="/wiki/Methanol" title="Methanol">methanol</a> and a range of other products,<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> such as metal <a href="/wiki/Carbonate" title="Carbonate">carbonates</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bicarbonate" title="Bicarbonate">bicarbonates</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Some carboxylic acid derivatives such as <a href="/wiki/Sodium_salicylate" title="Sodium salicylate">sodium salicylate</a> are prepared using CO<sub>2</sub> by the <a href="/wiki/Kolbe-Schmitt_reaction" class="mw-redirect" title="Kolbe-Schmitt reaction">Kolbe-Schmitt reaction</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup></p>
<p>In addition to conventional processes using CO<sub>2</sub> for chemical production, electrochemical methods are also being explored at a research level. In particular, the use of renewable energy for production of fuels from CO<sub>2</sub> (such as methanol) is attractive as this could result in fuels that could be easily transported and used within conventional combustion technologies but have no net CO<sub>2</sub> emissions.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Foods">Foods</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_dioxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Foods">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Carbon dioxide is a <a href="/wiki/Food_additive" title="Food additive">food additive</a> used as a propellant and acidity regulator in the food industry. It is approved for usage in the EU<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> (listed as <a href="/wiki/E_number" title="E number">E number</a> E290), US<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> and Australia and New Zealand<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> (listed by its INS number 290).</p>
<p>A candy called <a href="/wiki/Pop_Rocks" title="Pop Rocks">Pop Rocks</a> is pressurized with carbon dioxide gas at about 4 x 10<sup>6</sup> Pa (40 bar, 580 psi). When placed in the mouth, it dissolves (just like other hard candy) and releases the gas bubbles with an audible pop.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Leavening_agent" title="Leavening agent">Leavening agents</a> cause dough to rise by producing carbon dioxide. <a href="/wiki/Baker%27s_yeast" title="Baker's yeast">Baker's yeast</a> produces carbon dioxide by fermentation of sugars within the dough, while chemical leaveners such as <a href="/wiki/Baking_powder" title="Baking powder">baking powder</a> and <a href="/wiki/Baking_soda" class="mw-redirect" title="Baking soda">baking soda</a> release carbon dioxide when heated or if exposed to <a href="/wiki/Acid" title="Acid">acids</a>.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Beverages">Beverages</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_dioxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Beverages">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Carbon dioxide is used to produce <a href="/wiki/Carbonation" title="Carbonation">carbonated</a> <a href="/wiki/Soft_drink" title="Soft drink">soft drinks</a> and <a href="/wiki/Soda_water" class="mw-redirect" title="Soda water">soda water</a>. Traditionally, the carbonation of beer and sparkling wine came about through natural fermentation, but many manufacturers carbonate these drinks with carbon dioxide recovered from the fermentation process. In the case of bottled and kegged beer, the most common method used is carbonation with recycled carbon dioxide. With the exception of British <a href="/wiki/Real_Ale" class="mw-redirect" title="Real Ale">Real Ale</a>, draught beer is usually transferred from kegs in a cold room or cellar to dispensing taps on the bar using pressurized carbon dioxide, sometimes mixed with nitrogen.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Wine_making">Wine making</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_dioxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Wine making">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Carbon dioxide in the form of <a href="/wiki/Dry_ice" title="Dry ice">dry ice</a> is often used in the <a href="/wiki/Wine_making" class="mw-redirect" title="Wine making">wine making</a> process to cool down clusters of <a href="/wiki/Grape" title="Grape">grapes</a> quickly after picking to help prevent spontaneous <a href="/wiki/Fermentation_(wine)" class="mw-redirect" title="Fermentation (wine)">fermentation</a> by wild <a href="/wiki/Yeast_(wine)" class="mw-redirect" title="Yeast (wine)">yeast</a>. The main advantage of using dry ice over regular water ice is that it cools the grapes without adding any additional water that may decrease the <a href="/wiki/Sugar" title="Sugar">sugar</a> concentration in the <a href="/wiki/Grape_must" class="mw-redirect" title="Grape must">grape must</a>, and therefore also decrease the <a href="/wiki/Alcohol" title="Alcohol">alcohol</a> concentration in the finished wine.</p>
<p>Dry ice is used during the <a href="/w/index.php?title=Cold_soak&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Cold soak (page does not exist)">cold soak</a> phase of the wine making process to keep grapes cool. The carbon dioxide gas that results from the sublimation of the dry ice tends to settle to the bottom of tanks because it is denser than air. The settled carbon dioxide gas creates a hypoxic environment which helps to prevent bacteria from growing on the grapes until it is time to start the fermentation with the desired strain of yeast.</p>
<p>Carbon dioxide is also used to create a hypoxic environment for <a href="/wiki/Carbonic_maceration" title="Carbonic maceration">carbonic maceration</a>, the process used to produce <a href="/wiki/Beaujolais" title="Beaujolais">Beaujolais</a> wine.</p>
<p>Carbon dioxide is sometimes used to top up wine bottles or other <a href="/wiki/Storage_(wine)" class="mw-redirect" title="Storage (wine)">storage</a> vessels such as barrels to prevent oxidation, though it has the problem that it can dissolve into the wine, making a previously still wine slightly fizzy. For this reason, other gases such as <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a> or <a href="/wiki/Argon" title="Argon">argon</a> are preferred for this process by professional wine makers.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Inert_gas">Inert gas</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_dioxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Inert gas">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>It is one of the most commonly used compressed gases for pneumatic (pressurized gas) systems in portable pressure tools. Carbon dioxide is also used as an atmosphere for <a href="/wiki/Welding" title="Welding">welding</a>, although in the welding arc, it reacts to <a href="/wiki/Oxidation" class="mw-redirect" title="Oxidation">oxidize</a> most metals. Use in the automotive industry is common despite significant evidence that welds made in carbon dioxide are more <a href="/wiki/Brittle" class="mw-redirect" title="Brittle">brittle</a> than those made in more inert atmospheres, and that such weld joints deteriorate over time because of the formation of carbonic acid.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> It is used as a welding gas primarily because it is much less expensive than more inert gases such as <a href="/wiki/Argon" title="Argon">argon</a> or <a href="/wiki/Helium" title="Helium">helium</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="why not use nitrogen? (July 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> When used for <a href="/wiki/MIG_welding" class="mw-redirect" title="MIG welding">MIG welding</a>, CO<sub>2</sub> use is sometimes referred to as MAG welding, for Metal Active Gas, as CO<sub>2</sub> can react at these high temperatures. It tends to produce a hotter puddle than truly inert atmospheres, improving the flow characteristics. Although, this may be due to atmospheric reactions occurring at the puddle site. This is usually the opposite of the desired effect when welding, as it tends to embrittle the site, but may not be a problem for general mild steel welding, where ultimate ductility is not a major concern.</p>
<p>It is used in many consumer products that require pressurized gas because it is inexpensive and nonflammable, and because it undergoes a phase transition from gas to liquid at room temperature at an attainable pressure of approximately 60 <a href="/wiki/Bar_(unit)" title="Bar (unit)">bar</a> (870 psi, 59 atm), allowing far more carbon dioxide to fit in a given container than otherwise would. Life jackets often contain canisters of pressured carbon dioxide for quick inflation. Aluminium capsules of CO<sub>2</sub> are also sold as supplies of compressed gas for <a href="/wiki/Air_gun" title="Air gun">airguns</a>, <a href="/wiki/Paintball" title="Paintball">paintball</a> markers, inflating bicycle tires, and for making <a href="/wiki/Carbonated_water" title="Carbonated water">carbonated water</a>. Rapid vaporization of liquid carbon dioxide is used for blasting in coal mines. High concentrations of carbon dioxide can also be used to kill pests. Liquid carbon dioxide is used in <a href="/wiki/Supercritical_drying" title="Supercritical drying">supercritical drying</a> of some food products and technological materials, in the preparation of specimens for <a href="/wiki/Scanning_electron_microscopy" class="mw-redirect" title="Scanning electron microscopy">scanning electron microscopy</a> and in the <a href="/wiki/Decaffeination" title="Decaffeination">decaffeination</a> of <a href="/wiki/Coffee" title="Coffee">coffee</a> beans.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fire_extinguisher">Fire extinguisher</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_dioxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Fire extinguisher">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Carbon dioxide can be used to extinguish flames by flooding the environment around the flame with the gas. It does not itself react to extinguish the flame, but starves the flame of oxygen by displacing it. Some <a href="/wiki/Fire_extinguisher#Clean_agents_and_carbon_dioxide" title="Fire extinguisher">fire extinguishers</a>, especially those designed for electrical fires, contain liquid carbon dioxide under pressure. Carbon dioxide extinguishers work well on small flammable liquid and electrical fires, but not on ordinary combustible fires, because although it excludes oxygen, it does not cool the burning substances significantly and when the carbon dioxide disperses they are free to catch fire upon exposure to atmospheric oxygen. Their desirability in electrical fire stems from the fact that, unlike water or other chemical based methods, Carbon dioxide will not cause short circuits, leading to even more damage to equipment. Because it is a gas, it is also easy to dispense large amounts of the gas automatically in IT infrastructure rooms, where the fire itself might be hard to reach with more immediate methods because it is behind rack doors and inside of cases. Carbon dioxide has also been widely used as an extinguishing agent in fixed fire protection systems for local application of specific hazards and total flooding of a protected space.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> International Maritime Organization standards also recognize carbon dioxide systems for fire protection of ship holds and engine rooms. Carbon dioxide based fire protection systems have been linked to several deaths, because it can cause suffocation in sufficiently high concentrations. A review of CO<sub>2</sub> systems identified 51 incidents between 1975 and the date of the report, causing 72 deaths and 145 injuries.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Supercritical_CO2_as_solvent">Supercritical CO<sub>2</sub> as solvent</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_dioxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Supercritical CO2 as solvent">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Supercritical_carbon_dioxide" title="Supercritical carbon dioxide">Supercritical carbon dioxide</a></div>
<p>Liquid carbon dioxide is a good <a href="/wiki/Solvent" title="Solvent">solvent</a> for many <a href="/wiki/Lipophilic" class="mw-redirect" title="Lipophilic">lipophilic</a> <a href="/wiki/Organic_compound" title="Organic compound">organic compounds</a> and is used to remove <a href="/wiki/Caffeine" title="Caffeine">caffeine</a> from <a href="/wiki/Coffee" title="Coffee">coffee</a>. Carbon dioxide has attracted attention in the <a href="/wiki/Pharmaceutical" class="mw-redirect" title="Pharmaceutical">pharmaceutical</a> and other chemical processing industries as a less toxic alternative to more traditional solvents such as <a href="/wiki/Organochloride" title="Organochloride">organochlorides</a>. It is used by some <a href="/wiki/Dry_cleaning" title="Dry cleaning">dry cleaners</a> for this reason (see <a href="/wiki/Green_chemistry" title="Green chemistry">green chemistry</a>). It is used in the preparation of some <a href="/wiki/Aerogel#Production" title="Aerogel">aerogels</a> because of the properties of supercritical carbon dioxide.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Agricultural_and_biological_applications">Agricultural and biological applications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_dioxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Agricultural and biological applications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Plants require carbon dioxide to conduct <a href="/wiki/Photosynthesis" title="Photosynthesis">photosynthesis</a>. The atmospheres of greenhouses may (if of large size, must) be enriched with additional CO<sub>2</sub> to sustain and increase the rate of plant growth.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> At very high concentrations (100 times atmospheric concentration, or greater), carbon dioxide can be toxic to animal life, so raising the concentration to 10,000 ppm (1%) or higher for several hours will eliminate pests such as <a href="/wiki/Whitefly" title="Whitefly">whiteflies</a> and <a href="/wiki/Spider_mite" title="Spider mite">spider mites</a> in a greenhouse.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup></p>
<p>In medicine, up to 5% carbon dioxide (130 times atmospheric concentration) is added to <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a> for stimulation of breathing after <a href="/wiki/Apnea" title="Apnea">apnea</a> and to stabilize the <span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
2</span>/CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
2</span></span> balance in blood.</p>
<p>It has been proposed that carbon dioxide from power generation be bubbled into ponds to stimulate growth of algae that could then be converted into <a href="/wiki/Biodiesel" title="Biodiesel">biodiesel</a> fuel.<sup id="cite_ref-csmon_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-csmon-37">[37]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Oil_recovery">Oil recovery</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_dioxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Oil recovery">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Carbon dioxide is used in <a href="/wiki/Enhanced_oil_recovery" title="Enhanced oil recovery">enhanced oil recovery</a> where it is injected into or adjacent to producing oil wells, usually under <a href="/wiki/Supercritical_fluid" title="Supercritical fluid">supercritical</a> conditions, when it becomes miscible with the oil. This approach can increase original oil recovery by reducing residual oil saturation by between 7 per cent to 23 per cent additional to <a href="/wiki/Extraction_of_petroleum#Primary_recovery" title="Extraction of petroleum">primary extraction</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> It acts as both a pressurizing agent and, when dissolved into the underground <a href="/wiki/Crude_oil" class="mw-redirect" title="Crude oil">crude oil</a>, significantly reduces its viscosity, and changing surface chemistry enabling the oil to flow more rapidly through the reservoir to the removal well.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> In mature oil fields, extensive pipe networks are used to carry the carbon dioxide to the injection points.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bio_transformation_into_fuel">Bio transformation into fuel</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_dioxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Bio transformation into fuel">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Researchers have genetically modified a strain of the <a href="/wiki/Cyanobacterium" class="mw-redirect" title="Cyanobacterium">cyanobacterium</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Synechococcus" title="Synechococcus">Synechococcus elongatus</a></i> to produce the fuels <a href="/wiki/Isobutyraldehyde" title="Isobutyraldehyde">isobutyraldehyde</a> and <a href="/wiki/Isobutanol" title="Isobutanol">isobutanol</a> from CO<sub>2</sub> using photosynthesis.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Refrigerant">Refrigerant</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_dioxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Refrigerant">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Comparison_carbon_dioxide_water_phase_diagrams.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Comparison_carbon_dioxide_water_phase_diagrams.svg/400px-Comparison_carbon_dioxide_water_phase_diagrams.svg.png" width="400" height="184" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Comparison_carbon_dioxide_water_phase_diagrams.svg/600px-Comparison_carbon_dioxide_water_phase_diagrams.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Comparison_carbon_dioxide_water_phase_diagrams.svg/800px-Comparison_carbon_dioxide_water_phase_diagrams.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="235" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Comparison_carbon_dioxide_water_phase_diagrams.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Comparison of phase diagrams of carbon dioxide (red) and water (blue) as a log-lin chart with phase transitions points at 1 atmosphere</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Liquid and solid carbon dioxide are important <a href="/wiki/Refrigerant" title="Refrigerant">refrigerants</a>, especially in the food industry, where they are employed during the transportation and storage of ice cream and other frozen foods. Solid carbon dioxide is called "dry ice" and is used for small shipments where refrigeration equipment is not practical. Solid carbon dioxide is always below −78.5&#160;°C at regular atmospheric pressure, regardless of the air temperature.</p>
<p><span id="R744"></span> Liquid carbon dioxide (industry nomenclature R744 or R-744) was used as a refrigerant prior to the discovery of <a href="/wiki/Dichlorodifluoromethane" title="Dichlorodifluoromethane">R-12</a> and may enjoy a renaissance due to the fact that <a href="/wiki/R134a" class="mw-redirect" title="R134a">R134a</a> contributes to <a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">climate change</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="so does carbon dioxide. What point is being made here? (June 2015)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Its physical properties are highly favorable for cooling, refrigeration, and heating purposes, having a high volumetric cooling capacity. Due to the need to operate at pressures of up to 130 <a href="/wiki/Bar_(unit)" title="Bar (unit)">bar</a> (1880 <a href="/wiki/Pounds_per_square_inch" title="Pounds per square inch">psi</a>), CO<sub>2</sub> systems require highly resistant components that have already been developed for mass production in many sectors. In automobile air conditioning, in more than 90% of all driving conditions for latitudes higher than 50°, R744 operates more efficiently than systems using R134a. Its environmental advantages (<a href="/wiki/Global_warming_potential" title="Global warming potential">GWP</a> of 1, non-ozone depleting, non-toxic, non-flammable) could make it the future working fluid to replace current HFCs in cars, supermarkets, and heat pump water heaters, among others. <a href="/wiki/Coca-Cola" title="Coca-Cola">Coca-Cola</a> has fielded CO<sub>2</sub>-based beverage coolers and the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Army" title="United States Army">U.S. Army</a> is interested in CO<sub>2</sub> refrigeration and heating technology.<sup id="cite_ref-ccref1_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ccref1-41">[41]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-usforces_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-usforces-42">[42]</a></sup></p>
<p>The global automobile industry is expected to decide on the next-generation refrigerant in car air conditioning. CO<sub>2</sub> is one discussed option.(see <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_automotive_air_conditioning" title="Sustainable automotive air conditioning">Sustainable automotive air conditioning</a>)</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Coal_bed_methane_recovery">Coal bed methane recovery</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_dioxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Coal bed methane recovery">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Enhanced_coal_bed_methane_recovery" title="Enhanced coal bed methane recovery">enhanced coal bed methane recovery</a>, carbon dioxide would be pumped into the coal seam to displace methane, as opposed to current methods which primarily rely on the removal of water (to reduce pressure) to make the coal seam release its trapped methane.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Minor_uses">Minor uses</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_dioxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Minor uses">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Carbon_Dioxide_Laser_At_The_Laser_Effects_Test_Facility.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Carbon_Dioxide_Laser_At_The_Laser_Effects_Test_Facility.jpg/300px-Carbon_Dioxide_Laser_At_The_Laser_Effects_Test_Facility.jpg" width="300" height="169" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Carbon_Dioxide_Laser_At_The_Laser_Effects_Test_Facility.jpg/450px-Carbon_Dioxide_Laser_At_The_Laser_Effects_Test_Facility.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Carbon_Dioxide_Laser_At_The_Laser_Effects_Test_Facility.jpg/600px-Carbon_Dioxide_Laser_At_The_Laser_Effects_Test_Facility.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1204" data-file-height="680" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Carbon_Dioxide_Laser_At_The_Laser_Effects_Test_Facility.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide_laser" title="Carbon dioxide laser">carbon dioxide laser</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Carbon dioxide is the <a href="/wiki/Active_laser_medium" title="Active laser medium">lasing medium</a> in a <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide_laser" title="Carbon dioxide laser">carbon dioxide laser</a>, which is one of the earliest type of lasers.</p>
<p>Carbon dioxide can be used as a means of controlling the <a href="/wiki/PH" title="PH">pH</a> of swimming pools, by continuously adding gas to the water, thus keeping the pH from rising. Among the advantages of this is the avoidance of handling (more hazardous) acids. Similarly, it is also used in the maintaining <a href="/wiki/Reef_aquarium" title="Reef aquarium">reef aquaria</a>, where it is commonly used in <a href="/wiki/Calcium_reactor" title="Calcium reactor">calcium reactors</a> to temporarily lower the pH of water being passed over <a href="/wiki/Calcium_carbonate" title="Calcium carbonate">calcium carbonate</a> in order to allow the calcium carbonate to dissolve into the water more freely where it is used by some <a href="/wiki/Coral" title="Coral">corals</a> to build their skeleton.</p>
<p>Used as the primary coolant in the British <a href="/wiki/Advanced_gas-cooled_reactor" title="Advanced gas-cooled reactor">advanced gas-cooled reactor</a> for nuclear power generation.</p>
<p>Carbon dioxide induction is commonly used for the euthanasia of laboratory research animals. Methods to administer CO<sub>2</sub> include placing animals directly into a closed, prefilled chamber containing CO<sub>2</sub>, or exposure to a gradually increasing concentration of CO<sub>2</sub>. In 2013, the <a href="/wiki/American_Veterinary_Medical_Association" title="American Veterinary Medical Association">American Veterinary Medical Association</a> issued new guidelines for carbon dioxide induction, stating that a displacement rate of 10% to 30% of the <a href="/wiki/Gas_chamber" title="Gas chamber">gas chamber</a> volume per minute is optimal for the humane euthanization of small rodents.<sup id="cite_ref-2013_AVMA_Guidelines_for_the_Euthanasia_of_Animals:_2013_Edition_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2013_AVMA_Guidelines_for_the_Euthanasia_of_Animals:_2013_Edition-44">[44]</a></sup></p>
<p>Carbon dioxide is also used in several related <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide_cleaning" title="Carbon dioxide cleaning">cleaning and surface preparation</a> techniques, and in Paintball Tanks.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="In_Earth.27s_atmosphere">In Earth's atmosphere</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_dioxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: In Earth's atmosphere">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide_in_Earth%27s_atmosphere" title="Carbon dioxide in Earth's atmosphere">Carbon dioxide in Earth's atmosphere</a> and <a href="/wiki/Carbon_cycle" title="Carbon cycle">Carbon cycle</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
