<div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Mauna_Loa_CO2_monthly_mean_concentration.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Mauna_Loa_CO2_monthly_mean_concentration.svg/280px-Mauna_Loa_CO2_monthly_mean_concentration.svg.png" width="280" height="280" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Mauna_Loa_CO2_monthly_mean_concentration.svg/420px-Mauna_Loa_CO2_monthly_mean_concentration.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Mauna_Loa_CO2_monthly_mean_concentration.svg/560px-Mauna_Loa_CO2_monthly_mean_concentration.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="512" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Mauna_Loa_CO2_monthly_mean_concentration.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Keeling_Curve" title="Keeling Curve">Keeling Curve</a> of atmospheric CO<sub>2</sub> concentrations measured at <a href="/wiki/Mauna_Loa_Observatory" title="Mauna Loa Observatory">Mauna Loa Observatory</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Carbon dioxide in <a href="/wiki/Earth%27s_atmosphere" class="mw-redirect" title="Earth's atmosphere">Earth's atmosphere</a> is a <a href="/wiki/Trace_gas" title="Trace gas">trace gas</a>, currently (early 2016) having an average concentration of 402 parts per million by volume<sup id="cite_ref-NOAA_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NOAA-3">[3]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-theguardian_mar_2016_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-theguardian_mar_2016-45">[45]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NOAA_mar_10_2016_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NOAA_mar_10_2016-46">[46]</a></sup> (or 611 parts per million by mass). Atmospheric concentrations of carbon dioxide fluctuate slightly with the seasons, falling during the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Hemisphere" title="Northern Hemisphere">Northern Hemisphere</a> spring and summer as plants consume the gas and rising during northern autumn and winter as plants go dormant or die and decay. Concentrations also vary on a regional basis, most strongly <a href="/wiki/Planetary_boundary_layer" title="Planetary boundary layer">near the ground</a> with much smaller variations aloft. In urban areas concentrations are generally higher<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> and indoors they can reach 10 times background levels.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:CO2_increase_rate.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/CO2_increase_rate.png/220px-CO2_increase_rate.png" width="220" height="191" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/CO2_increase_rate.png/330px-CO2_increase_rate.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/CO2_increase_rate.png/440px-CO2_increase_rate.png 2x" data-file-width="690" data-file-height="600" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:CO2_increase_rate.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Yearly increase of atmospheric CO<sub>2</sub>: In the 1960s, the average annual increase was 37% of the 2000–2007 average.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The concentration of carbon dioxide has risen due to human activities.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> Combustion of <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuel" title="Fossil fuel">fossil fuels</a> and <a href="/wiki/Deforestation" title="Deforestation">deforestation</a> have caused the atmospheric concentration of carbon dioxide to increase by about 43% since the beginning of the <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">age of industrialization</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-nonanews_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nonanews-50">[50]</a></sup> Most carbon dioxide from human activities is released from burning coal and other fossil fuels. Other human activities, including deforestation, biomass burning, and cement production also produce carbon dioxide. Human activities emit about 29 billion tons of carbon dioxide per year, while volcanoes emit between 0.2 and 0.3 billion tons.<sup id="cite_ref-climate.gov_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-climate.gov-51">[51]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup></p>
<p>Carbon dioxide is a <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas" title="Greenhouse gas">greenhouse gas</a>, absorbing and emitting infrared radiation at its two infrared-active vibrational frequencies (see the section "Structure and bonding" above). This causes carbon dioxide to warm the surface and lower atmosphere while cooling the upper atmosphere. Increases in atmospheric concentrations of CO<sub>2</sub> and other long-lived greenhouse gases such as methane, nitrous oxide and ozone have correspondingly strengthened their absorption and emission of infrared radiation, causing the rise in average global temperature since the mid-20th century. Carbon dioxide is of greatest concern because it exerts a larger overall warming influence than all of these other gases combined and because it has a long atmospheric lifetime (hundreds to thousands of years).</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:M15-162b-EarthAtmosphere-CarbonDioxide-FutureRoleInGlobalWarming-Simulation-20151109.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/M15-162b-EarthAtmosphere-CarbonDioxide-FutureRoleInGlobalWarming-Simulation-20151109.jpg/200px-M15-162b-EarthAtmosphere-CarbonDioxide-FutureRoleInGlobalWarming-Simulation-20151109.jpg" width="200" height="97" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/M15-162b-EarthAtmosphere-CarbonDioxide-FutureRoleInGlobalWarming-Simulation-20151109.jpg/300px-M15-162b-EarthAtmosphere-CarbonDioxide-FutureRoleInGlobalWarming-Simulation-20151109.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/M15-162b-EarthAtmosphere-CarbonDioxide-FutureRoleInGlobalWarming-Simulation-20151109.jpg/400px-M15-162b-EarthAtmosphere-CarbonDioxide-FutureRoleInGlobalWarming-Simulation-20151109.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1268" data-file-height="616" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:M15-162b-EarthAtmosphere-CarbonDioxide-FutureRoleInGlobalWarming-Simulation-20151109.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
CO<sub>2</sub> in <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere" title="Atmosphere">atmosphere</a> if <i>half</i> of global-warming emissions are <i>not</i> absorbed.<sup id="cite_ref-NASA-20151112-ab_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20151112-ab-53">[53]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NASA-20151112b_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20151112b-54">[54]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NYT-20151110_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20151110-55">[55]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-AP-20151109_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AP-20151109-56">[56]</a></sup><br />
(<a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a> <a href="/wiki/Computer_simulation" title="Computer simulation">computer simulation</a>).</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Not only do increasing carbon dioxide concentrations lead to increases in global surface temperature, but increasing global temperatures also cause increasing concentrations of carbon dioxide. This produces a <a href="/wiki/Positive_feedback" title="Positive feedback">positive feedback</a> for changes induced by other processes such as <a href="/wiki/Milankovitch_cycles" title="Milankovitch cycles">orbital cycles</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> Five hundred million years ago the carbon dioxide concentration was 20 times greater than today, decreasing to 4–5 times during the <a href="/wiki/Jurassic" title="Jurassic">Jurassic</a> period and then slowly declining with <a href="/wiki/Azolla_Event" class="mw-redirect" title="Azolla Event">a particularly swift reduction</a> occurring 49 million years ago.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup></p>
<p>Local concentrations of carbon dioxide can reach high values near strong sources, especially those that are isolated by surrounding terrain. At the Bossoleto hot spring near <a href="/wiki/Rapolano_Terme" title="Rapolano Terme">Rapolano Terme</a> in <a href="/wiki/Tuscany" title="Tuscany">Tuscany</a>, <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a>, situated in a bowl-shaped depression about 100&#160;m (330&#160;ft) in diameter, concentrations of CO<sub>2</sub> rise to above 75% overnight, sufficient to kill insects and small animals. After sunrise the gas is dispersed by convection during the day.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> High concentrations of CO<sub>2</sub> produced by disturbance of deep lake water saturated with CO<sub>2</sub> are thought to have caused 37 fatalities at <a href="/wiki/Lake_Monoun" title="Lake Monoun">Lake Monoun</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cameroon" title="Cameroon">Cameroon</a> in 1984 and 1700 casualties at <a href="/wiki/Lake_Nyos" title="Lake Nyos">Lake Nyos</a>, Cameroon in 1986.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup></p>
<p>Human-made carbon dioxide (CO<sub>2</sub>) continues to increase above levels not seen in hundreds of thousands of years. Currently, about half of the carbon dioxide released from the <a href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">burning of fossil fuels</a> remains in the <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere" title="Atmosphere">atmosphere</a> and is not absorbed by vegetation and the oceans.<sup id="cite_ref-NASA-20151112-ab_53-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20151112-ab-53">[53]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NASA-20151112b_54-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20151112b-54">[54]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NYT-20151110_55-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20151110-55">[55]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-AP-20151109_56-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AP-20151109-56">[56]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="In_the_oceans">In the oceans</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_dioxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: In the oceans">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Carbon_cycle" title="Carbon cycle">Carbon cycle</a></div>
<p>Carbon dioxide dissolves in the ocean to form <a href="/wiki/Carbonic_acid" title="Carbonic acid">carbonic acid</a> (H<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>), <a href="/wiki/Bicarbonate" title="Bicarbonate">bicarbonate</a> (HCO<sub>3</sub><sup>−</sup>) and <a href="/wiki/Carbonate" title="Carbonate">carbonate</a> (CO<sub>3</sub><sup>2−</sup>). There is about fifty times as much carbon dissolved in the oceans as exists in the atmosphere. The oceans act as an enormous <a href="/wiki/Carbon_sink" title="Carbon sink">carbon sink</a>, and have taken up about a third of CO<sub>2</sub> emitted by human activity.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup></p>
<p>As the concentration of carbon dioxide increases in the atmosphere, the increased uptake of carbon dioxide into the oceans is causing a measurable decrease in the pH of the oceans, which is referred to as <a href="/wiki/Ocean_acidification" title="Ocean acidification">ocean acidification</a>. This reduction in pH affects biological systems in the oceans, primarily oceanic <a href="/wiki/Calcification" title="Calcification">calcifying</a> organisms. These effects span the <a href="/wiki/Food_chain" title="Food chain">food chain</a> from <a href="/wiki/Autotroph" title="Autotroph">autotrophs</a> to <a href="/wiki/Heterotroph" title="Heterotroph">heterotrophs</a> and include organisms such as <a href="/wiki/Coccolithophore" title="Coccolithophore">coccolithophores</a>, <a href="/wiki/Coral" title="Coral">corals</a>, <a href="/wiki/Foraminifera" title="Foraminifera">foraminifera</a>, <a href="/wiki/Echinoderm" title="Echinoderm">echinoderms</a>, <a href="/wiki/Crustacea" class="mw-redirect" title="Crustacea">crustaceans</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mollusca" title="Mollusca">mollusks</a>. Under normal conditions, calcium carbonate is stable in surface waters since the carbonate ion is at <a href="/wiki/Supersaturation" title="Supersaturation">supersaturating</a> concentrations. However, as ocean pH falls, so does the concentration of this ion, and when carbonate becomes undersaturated, structures made of calcium carbonate are vulnerable to dissolution.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> Corals,<sup id="cite_ref-gatt98_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gatt98-64">[64]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-gatt99_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gatt99-65">[65]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-lan05_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lan05-66">[66]</a></sup> coccolithophore algae,<sup id="cite_ref-rieb00_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rieb00-67">[67]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-zond01_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-zond01-68">[68]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-zond02_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-zond02-69">[69]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-delille05_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-delille05-70">[70]</a></sup> coralline algae,<sup id="cite_ref-kuffner_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kuffner-71">[71]</a></sup> foraminifera,<sup id="cite_ref-catalyst-2007-09-13-OAtbgws_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-catalyst-2007-09-13-OAtbgws-72">[72]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Shellfish" title="Shellfish">shellfish</a><sup id="cite_ref-gaz07_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gaz07-73">[73]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Pteropod" class="mw-redirect" title="Pteropod">pteropods</a><sup id="cite_ref-comeau09_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-comeau09-74">[74]</a></sup> experience reduced calcification or enhanced dissolution when exposed to elevated <span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
2</span></span>.</p>
<p>Gas solubility decreases as the temperature of water increases (except when both pressure exceeds 300 bar and temperature exceeds 393 K, only found near deep geothermal vents)<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> and therefore the rate of uptake from the atmosphere decreases as ocean temperatures rise.</p>
<p>Most of the CO<sub>2</sub> taken up by the ocean, which is about 30% of the total released into the atmosphere,<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> forms carbonic acid in equilibrium with bicarbonate. Some of these chemical species are consumed by photosynthetic organisms that remove carbon from the cycle. Increased CO<sub>2</sub> in the atmosphere has led to decreasing <a href="/wiki/Alkalinity" title="Alkalinity">alkalinity</a> of seawater, and there is concern that this may adversely affect organisms living in the water. In particular, with decreasing alkalinity, the availability of carbonates for forming shells decreases,<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> although there's evidence of increased shell production by certain species under increased CO<sub>2</sub> content.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup></p>
<p>NOAA states in their May 2008 "State of the science fact sheet for <a href="/wiki/Ocean_acidification" title="Ocean acidification">ocean acidification</a>" that:<br />
"The oceans have absorbed about 50% of the carbon dioxide (CO<sub>2</sub>) released from the burning of fossil fuels, resulting in chemical reactions that lower ocean pH. This has caused an increase in hydrogen ion (acidity) of about 30% since the start of the industrial age through a process known as "ocean acidification." A growing number of studies have demonstrated adverse impacts on marine organisms, including:</p>
<ul>
<li>The rate at which reef-building corals produce their skeletons decreases, while production of numerous varieties of jellyfish increases.</li>
<li>The ability of marine algae and free-swimming zooplankton to maintain protective shells is reduced.</li>
<li>The survival of larval marine species, including commercial fish and shellfish, is reduced."</li>
</ul>
<p>Also, the Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change (IPCC) writes in their Climate Change 2007: Synthesis Report:<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup><br />
"The uptake of anthropogenic carbon since 1750 has led to the ocean becoming more acidic with an average decrease in pH of 0.1 units. Increasing atmospheric CO<sub>2</sub> concentrations lead to further acidification&#160;... While the effects of observed ocean acidification on the marine biosphere are as yet undocumented, the progressive acidification of oceans is expected to have negative impacts on marine shell-forming organisms (e.g. corals) and their dependent species."</p>
<p>Some marine calcifying organisms (including coral reefs) have been singled out by major research agencies, including NOAA, OSPAR commission, NANOOS and the IPCC, because their most current research shows that ocean acidification should be expected to impact them negatively.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup></p>
<p>Carbon dioxide is also introduced into the oceans through hydrothermal vents. The <i>Champagne</i> hydrothermal vent, found at the Northwest Eifuku volcano at <a href="/wiki/Marianas_Trench_Marine_National_Monument" title="Marianas Trench Marine National Monument">Marianas Trench Marine National Monument</a>, produces almost pure liquid carbon dioxide, one of only two known sites in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Biological_role">Biological role</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_dioxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Biological role">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Carbon dioxide is an end product of <a href="/wiki/Cellular_respiration" title="Cellular respiration">cellular respiration</a> in organisms that obtain energy by breaking down sugars, fats and <a href="/wiki/Amino_acid" title="Amino acid">amino acids</a> with <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a> as part of their <a href="/wiki/Metabolism" title="Metabolism">metabolism</a>. This includes all plants, algae and animals and <a href="/wiki/Aerobic_respiration" class="mw-redirect" title="Aerobic respiration">aerobic</a> fungi and bacteria. In <a href="/wiki/Vertebrate" title="Vertebrate">vertebrates</a>, the carbon dioxide travels in the blood from the body's tissues to the skin (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Amphibian" title="Amphibian">amphibians</a>) or the gills (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Fish" title="Fish">fish</a>), from where it dissolves in the water, or to the lungs from where it is exhaled. During active photosynthesis, <a href="/wiki/Compensation_point" title="Compensation point">plants can absorb more carbon dioxide from the atmosphere than they release</a> in respiration.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Photosynthesis_and_carbon_fixation">Photosynthesis and carbon fixation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_dioxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Photosynthesis and carbon fixation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Auto-and_heterotrophs.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Auto-and_heterotrophs.png/200px-Auto-and_heterotrophs.png" width="200" height="233" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Auto-and_heterotrophs.png/300px-Auto-and_heterotrophs.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Auto-and_heterotrophs.png/400px-Auto-and_heterotrophs.png 2x" data-file-width="2722" data-file-height="3167" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Auto-and_heterotrophs.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Overview of photosynthesis and respiration. Carbon dioxide (at right), together with water, form oxygen and organic compounds (at left) by <a href="/wiki/Photosynthesis" title="Photosynthesis"><span style="color:green;">photosynthesis</span></a>, which can be <a href="/wiki/Cellular_respiration" title="Cellular respiration"><span style="color:red;">respired</span></a> to water and (CO<sub>2</sub>).</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Calvin-cycle4.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Calvin-cycle4.svg/300px-Calvin-cycle4.svg.png" width="300" height="275" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Calvin-cycle4.svg/450px-Calvin-cycle4.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Calvin-cycle4.svg/600px-Calvin-cycle4.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="836" data-file-height="766" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Calvin-cycle4.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<b>Figure 2</b>. Overview of the <a href="/wiki/Calvin_cycle" class="mw-redirect" title="Calvin cycle">Calvin cycle</a> and carbon fixation</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Carbon_fixation" title="Carbon fixation">Carbon fixation</a> is a biochemical process by which atmospheric carbon dioxide is incorporated by <a href="/wiki/Plant" title="Plant">plants</a>, <a href="/wiki/Algae" title="Algae">algae</a> and (<a href="/wiki/Cyanobacteria" title="Cyanobacteria">cyanobacteria</a>) into <a href="/wiki/Fuel" title="Fuel">energy-rich</a> organic <a href="/wiki/Molecule" title="Molecule">molecules</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Glucose" title="Glucose">glucose</a>, thus creating their own food by <a href="/wiki/Photosynthesis" title="Photosynthesis">photosynthesis</a>. Photosynthesis uses carbon dioxide and <a href="/wiki/Water" title="Water">water</a> to produce <a href="/wiki/Sugar" title="Sugar">sugars</a> from which other <a href="/wiki/Organic_compound" title="Organic compound">organic compounds</a> can be constructed, and <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a> is produced as a by-product.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/RuBisCO" title="RuBisCO">Ribulose-1,5-bisphosphate carboxylase oxygenase</a>, commonly abbreviated to RuBisCO, is the <a href="/wiki/Enzyme" title="Enzyme">enzyme</a> involved in the first major step of carbon fixation, the production of two molecules of <a href="/wiki/3-phosphoglycerate" class="mw-redirect" title="3-phosphoglycerate">3-phosphoglycerate</a> from CO<sub>2</sub> and <a href="/wiki/Ribulose_bisphosphate" class="mw-redirect" title="Ribulose bisphosphate">ribulose bisphosphate</a>, as shown in the diagram at left.</p>
<p>RuBisCO is thought to be the single most abundant protein on Earth.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Phototroph" title="Phototroph">Phototrophs</a> use the products of their photosynthesis as internal food sources and as raw material for the <a href="/wiki/Biosynthesis" title="Biosynthesis">biosynthesis</a> of more complex organic molecules, such as <a href="/wiki/Polysaccharides" class="mw-redirect" title="Polysaccharides">polysaccharides</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nucleic_acid" title="Nucleic acid">nucleic acids</a> and <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">proteins</a>. These are used for their own growth, and also as the basis of the <a href="/wiki/Food_chain" title="Food chain">food chains</a> and webs that feed other organisms, including animals such as ourselves. Some important phototrophs, the <a href="/wiki/Coccolithophore" title="Coccolithophore">coccolithophores</a> synthesise hard <a href="/wiki/Calcium_carbonate" title="Calcium carbonate">calcium carbonate</a> scales.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> A globally significant species of coccolithophore is <i><a href="/wiki/Emiliania_huxleyi" title="Emiliania huxleyi">Emiliania huxleyi</a></i> whose <a href="/wiki/Calcite" title="Calcite">calcite</a> scales have formed the basis of many <a href="/wiki/Sedimentary_rock" title="Sedimentary rock">sedimentary rocks</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Limestone" title="Limestone">limestone</a>, where what was previously atmospheric carbon can remain fixed for geological timescales.</p>
<p>Plants can grow as much as 50 percent faster in concentrations of 1,000 ppm CO<sub>2</sub> when compared with ambient conditions, though this assumes no change in climate and no limitation on other nutrients.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> Elevated CO<sub>2</sub> levels cause increased growth reflected in the harvestable yield of crops, with wheat, rice and soybean all showing increases in yield of 12–14% under elevated CO<sub>2</sub> in FACE experiments.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup></p>
<p>Increased atmospheric CO<sub>2</sub> concentrations result in fewer stomata developing on plants<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> which leads to reduced water usage and increased <a href="/wiki/Water-use_efficiency" title="Water-use efficiency">water-use efficiency</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> Studies using <a href="/wiki/Free-Air_Concentration_Enrichment" class="mw-redirect" title="Free-Air Concentration Enrichment">FACE</a> have shown that CO<sub>2</sub> enrichment leads to decreased concentrations of micronutrients in crop plants.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> This may have knock-on effects on other parts of <a href="/wiki/Ecosystem" title="Ecosystem">ecosystems</a> as herbivores will need to eat more food to gain the same amount of protein.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup></p>
<p>The concentration of secondary <a href="/wiki/Metabolites" class="mw-redirect" title="Metabolites">metabolites</a> such as phenylpropanoids and flavonoids can also be altered in plants exposed to high concentrations of CO<sub>2</sub>.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup></p>
<p>Plants also emit CO<sub>2</sub> during respiration, and so the majority of plants and algae, which use <a href="/wiki/C3_photosynthesis" class="mw-redirect" title="C3 photosynthesis">C3 photosynthesis</a>, are only net absorbers during the day. Though a growing forest will absorb many tons of CO<sub>2</sub> each year, a mature forest will produce as much CO<sub>2</sub> from respiration and decomposition of dead specimens (e.g., fallen branches) as is used in photosynthesis in growing plants.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> Contrary to the long-standing view that they are carbon neutral, mature forests can continue to accumulate carbon<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> and remain valuable <a href="/wiki/Carbon_sink" title="Carbon sink">carbon sinks</a>, helping to maintain the carbon balance of Earth's atmosphere. Additionally, and crucially to life on earth, photosynthesis by phytoplankton consumes dissolved CO<sub>2</sub> in the upper ocean and thereby promotes the absorption of CO<sub>2</sub> from the atmosphere.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Toxicity">Toxicity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_dioxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Toxicity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide_poisoning" class="mw-redirect" title="Carbon dioxide poisoning">Carbon dioxide poisoning</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:272px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Main_symptoms_of_carbon_dioxide_toxicity.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Main_symptoms_of_carbon_dioxide_toxicity.svg/270px-Main_symptoms_of_carbon_dioxide_toxicity.svg.png" width="270" height="233" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Main_symptoms_of_carbon_dioxide_toxicity.svg/405px-Main_symptoms_of_carbon_dioxide_toxicity.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Main_symptoms_of_carbon_dioxide_toxicity.svg/540px-Main_symptoms_of_carbon_dioxide_toxicity.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1196" data-file-height="1033" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Main_symptoms_of_carbon_dioxide_toxicity.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Main symptoms of carbon dioxide toxicity, by increasing <a href="/wiki/Volume_percent" class="mw-redirect" title="Volume percent">volume percent</a> in air.<sup id="cite_ref-friedman_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-friedman-96">[96]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Carbon dioxide content in fresh air (averaged between sea-level and 10 kPa level, i.e., about 30&#160;km (19&#160;mi) altitude) varies between 0.036% (360 ppm) and 0.041% (410 ppm), depending on the location.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="what date/year are these figures for? (June 2014)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>CO<sub>2</sub> is an <a href="/wiki/Asphyxiant_gas" title="Asphyxiant gas">asphyxiant gas</a> and not classified as toxic or harmful in accordance with <a href="/wiki/Globally_Harmonized_System_of_Classification_and_Labelling_of_Chemicals" title="Globally Harmonized System of Classification and Labelling of Chemicals">Globally Harmonized System of Classification and Labelling of Chemicals standards</a> of <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Economic_Commission_for_Europe" title="United Nations Economic Commission for Europe">United Nations Economic Commission for Europe</a> by using the <a href="/wiki/OECD_Guidelines_for_the_Testing_of_Chemicals" title="OECD Guidelines for the Testing of Chemicals">OECD Guidelines for the Testing of Chemicals</a>. In concentrations up to 1% (10,000 ppm), it will make some people feel drowsy and give the lungs a stuffy feeling.<sup id="cite_ref-friedman_96-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-friedman-96">[96]</a></sup> Concentrations of 7% to 10% (70,000 to 100,000 ppm) may cause suffocation, even in the presence of sufficient oxygen, manifesting as dizziness, headache, visual and hearing dysfunction, and unconsciousness within a few minutes to an hour.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> The physiological effects of acute carbon dioxide exposure are grouped together under the term <a href="/wiki/Hypercapnia" title="Hypercapnia">hypercapnia</a>, a subset of <a href="/wiki/Asphyxiant_gas" title="Asphyxiant gas">asphyxiation</a>.</p>
<p>Because it is heavier than air, in locations where the gas seeps from the ground (due to sub-surface volcanic or geothermal activity) in relatively high concentrations, without the dispersing effects of wind, it can collect in sheltered/pocketed locations below average ground level, causing animals located therein to be suffocated. Carrion feeders attracted to the carcasses are then also killed. Children have been killed in the same way near the city of <a href="/wiki/Goma" title="Goma">Goma</a> by CO<sub>2</sub> emissions from the nearby volcano <a href="/wiki/Mt._Nyiragongo" class="mw-redirect" title="Mt. Nyiragongo">Mt. Nyiragongo</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Swahili_language" title="Swahili language">Swahili</a> term for this phenomenon is '<a href="/wiki/Mazuku" title="Mazuku">mazuku</a>'.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Apollo13_apparatus.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Apollo13_apparatus.jpg/220px-Apollo13_apparatus.jpg" width="220" height="220" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Apollo13_apparatus.jpg/330px-Apollo13_apparatus.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Apollo13_apparatus.jpg/440px-Apollo13_apparatus.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3888" data-file-height="3888" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Apollo13_apparatus.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Rising levels of CO<sub>2</sub> threatened the <a href="/wiki/Apollo_13" title="Apollo 13">Apollo 13</a> astronauts who had to adapt cartridges from the command module to supply the <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide_scrubber" title="Carbon dioxide scrubber">carbon dioxide scrubber</a> in the lunar module, which they used as a lifeboat.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Adaptation to increased concentrations of CO<sub>2</sub> occurs in humans, including <a href="/wiki/Respiratory_adaptation" title="Respiratory adaptation">modified breathing</a> and kidney bicarbonate production, in order to balance the effects of blood acidification (<a href="/wiki/Acidosis" title="Acidosis">acidosis</a>). Several studies suggested that 2.0 percent inspired concentrations could be used for closed air spaces (e.g. a <a href="/wiki/Submarine" title="Submarine">submarine</a>) since the adaptation is physiological and reversible, as decrement in performance or in normal physical activity does not happen at this level of exposure for five days.<sup id="cite_ref-Glatte_Jr_H._A..2C_Motsay_G._J..2C_Welch_B._E._1967_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Glatte_Jr_H._A..2C_Motsay_G._J..2C_Welch_B._E._1967-100">[100]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> Yet, other studies show a decrease in cognitive function even at much lower levels.<sup id="cite_ref-pollutant2012_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pollutant2012-102">[102]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-scores2016_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-scores2016-103">[103]</a></sup> Also, with ongoing respiratory acidosis, adaptation or <a href="/wiki/Acidosis" title="Acidosis">compensatory mechanisms will be unable to reverse such condition</a>.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Below_1.25">Below 1%</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_dioxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Below 1%">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>There are few studies of the health effects of long-term continuous CO<sub>2</sub> exposure on humans and animals at levels below 1% and there is potentially a significant risk to humans in the near future with rising atmospheric CO<sub>2</sub> levels associated with <a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">climate change</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> Occupational CO<sub>2</sub> exposure limits have been set in the United States at 0.5% (5000 ppm) for an eight-hour period.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> At this CO<sub>2</sub> concentration, <a href="/wiki/International_Space_Station" title="International Space Station">International Space Station</a> crew experienced headaches, lethargy, mental slowness, emotional irritation, and sleep disruption.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> Studies in animals at 0.5% CO<sub>2</sub> have demonstrated kidney calcification and bone loss after eight weeks of exposure.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup> A study of humans exposed in 2.5 hour sessions demonstrated significant effects on cognitive abilities at concentrations as low as 0.1% (1000ppm) CO<sub>2</sub> likely due to CO<sub>2</sub> induced increases in cerebral blood flow.<sup id="cite_ref-pollutant2012_102-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pollutant2012-102">[102]</a></sup> Another study observed a decline in basic activity level and information usage at 1000 ppm, when compared to 500 ppm.<sup id="cite_ref-scores2016_103-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-scores2016-103">[103]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Ventilation">Ventilation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_dioxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Ventilation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Poor ventilation is one of the main causes of excessive CO<sub>2</sub> concentrations in closed spaces. Carbon dioxide differential above outdoor concentrations at steady state conditions (when the occupancy and ventilation system operation are sufficiently long that CO<sub>2</sub> concentration has stabilized) are sometimes used to estimate ventilation rates per person.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Higher CO<sub>2</sub> concentrations are associated with occupant health, comfort and performance degradation.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> <a href="/wiki/ASHRAE" title="ASHRAE">ASHRAE</a> Standard 62.1–2007 ventilation rates may result in indoor levels up to 2,100 ppm above ambient outdoor conditions. Thus if the outdoor concentration is 400 ppm, indoor concentrations may reach 2,500 ppm with ventilation rates that meet this industry consensus standard. Concentrations in poorly ventilated spaces can be found even higher than this (range of 3,000 or 4,000).</p>
<p>Miners, who are particularly vulnerable to gas exposure due to an insufficient ventilation, referred to mixtures of carbon dioxide and nitrogen as "<a href="/wiki/Blackdamp" title="Blackdamp">blackdamp</a>," "choke damp" or "stythe." Before more effective technologies were developed, <a href="/wiki/Miners" class="mw-redirect" title="Miners">miners</a> would frequently monitor for dangerous levels of blackdamp and other gases in mine shafts by bringing a caged <a href="/wiki/Domestic_Canary" class="mw-redirect" title="Domestic Canary">canary</a> with them as they worked. The canary is more sensitive to asphyxiant gases than humans, and as it became unconscious would stop singing and fall off its perch. The <a href="/wiki/Davy_lamp" title="Davy lamp">Davy lamp</a> could also detect high levels of blackdamp (which sinks, and collects near the floor) by burning less brightly, while <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a>, another suffocating gas and explosion risk, would make the lamp burn more brightly.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Human_physiology">Human physiology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_dioxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Human physiology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Content">Content</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_dioxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Content">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The body produces approximately 2.3 pounds (1.0&#160;kg) of carbon dioxide per day per person,<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup> containing 0.63 pounds (290&#160;g) of carbon. <span id="partial_pressure"></span> In humans, this carbon dioxide is carried through the <a href="/wiki/Venous_system" class="mw-redirect" title="Venous system">venous system</a> and is breathed out through the lungs, resulting in lower concentrations in the <a href="/wiki/Arteries" class="mw-redirect" title="Arteries">arteries</a>. The carbon dioxide content of the blood is often given as the <a href="/wiki/Partial_pressure" title="Partial pressure">partial pressure</a>, which is the pressure which carbon dioxide would have had if it alone occupied the volume.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup></p>
<p>In humans, the carbon dioxide contents are as follows:</p>
<center>
<table class="wikitable">
<caption><a href="/wiki/Reference_range" title="Reference range">Reference ranges</a> or averages for <a href="/wiki/Partial_pressure_of_carbon_dioxide" class="mw-redirect" title="Partial pressure of carbon dioxide">partial pressures of carbon dioxide</a> (abbreviated PCO<sub>2</sub>)</caption>
<tr>
<th>Unit</th>
<th><a href="/wiki/Vein" title="Vein">Venous</a> blood gas</th>

<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Transport_in_the_blood">Transport in the blood</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_dioxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Transport in the blood">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>CO<sub>2</sub> is carried in blood in three different ways. (The exact percentages vary depending whether it is arterial or venous blood).</p>
<ul>
<li>Most of it (about 70% to 80%) is converted to <a href="/wiki/Bicarbonate" title="Bicarbonate">bicarbonate</a> ions <span class="chemf nowrap">HCO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">−<br />
3</span></span> by the enzyme <a href="/wiki/Carbonic_anhydrase" title="Carbonic anhydrase">carbonic anhydrase</a> in the red blood cells,<sup id="cite_ref-solarnav_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-solarnav-112">[112]</a></sup> by the reaction CO<sub>2</sub> + H<sub>2</sub>O → H<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> → H<sup>+</sup> + <span class="chemf nowrap">HCO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">−<br />
3</span></span>.</li>
<li>5% – 10% is dissolved in the <a href="/wiki/Blood_plasma" title="Blood plasma">plasma</a><sup id="cite_ref-solarnav_112-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-solarnav-112">[112]</a></sup></li>
<li>5% – 10% is bound to <a href="/wiki/Hemoglobin" title="Hemoglobin">hemoglobin</a> as <a href="/wiki/Carbamino" title="Carbamino">carbamino</a> compounds<sup id="cite_ref-solarnav_112-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-solarnav-112">[112]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<p><a href="/wiki/Hemoglobin" title="Hemoglobin">Hemoglobin</a>, the main oxygen-carrying molecule in <a href="/wiki/Red_blood_cell" title="Red blood cell">red blood cells</a>, carries both oxygen and carbon dioxide. However, the CO<sub>2</sub> bound to hemoglobin does not bind to the same site as oxygen. Instead, it combines with the N-terminal groups on the four globin chains. However, because of <a href="/wiki/Allosteric_regulation" title="Allosteric regulation">allosteric</a> effects on the hemoglobin molecule, the binding of CO<sub>2</sub> decreases the amount of oxygen that is bound for a given partial pressure of oxygen. The decreased binding to carbon dioxide in the blood due to increased oxygen levels is known as the <a href="/wiki/Haldane_Effect" class="mw-redirect" title="Haldane Effect">Haldane Effect</a>, and is important in the transport of carbon dioxide from the tissues to the lungs. Conversely, a rise in the partial pressure of CO<sub>2</sub> or a lower pH will cause offloading of oxygen from hemoglobin, which is known as the <a href="/wiki/Bohr_Effect" class="mw-redirect" title="Bohr Effect">Bohr Effect</a>.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Regulation_of_respiration">Regulation of respiration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_dioxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Regulation of respiration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image">
<div style="width:52px"><a href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" width="50" height="39" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="399" /></a></div>
</td>
<td class="mbox-text"><span class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs additional citations for <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">verification</a></b>. <span class="hide-when-compact">Please help <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Carbon_dioxide&amp;action=edit">improve this article</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and removed.</span> <small><i>(June 2014)</i></small> <small class="hide-when-compact"><i>(<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></span></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>Carbon dioxide is one of the mediators of local <a href="/wiki/Autoregulation" title="Autoregulation">autoregulation</a> of blood supply. If its concentration is high, the <a href="/wiki/Capillaries" class="mw-redirect" title="Capillaries">capillaries</a> expand to allow a greater blood flow to that tissue.</p>
<p>Bicarbonate ions are crucial for regulating blood pH. A person's breathing rate influences the level of CO<sub>2</sub> in their blood. Breathing that is too slow or shallow causes <a href="/wiki/Respiratory_acidosis" title="Respiratory acidosis">respiratory acidosis</a>, while breathing that is too rapid leads to <a href="/wiki/Hyperventilation" title="Hyperventilation">hyperventilation</a>, which can cause <a href="/wiki/Alkalosis" title="Alkalosis">respiratory alkalosis</a>.</p>
<p>Although the body requires oxygen for metabolism, low oxygen levels normally do not stimulate breathing. Rather, breathing is stimulated by higher carbon dioxide levels. As a result, breathing low-pressure air or a gas mixture with no oxygen at all (such as pure nitrogen) can lead to loss of consciousness without ever experiencing <a href="/wiki/Air_hunger" class="mw-redirect" title="Air hunger">air hunger</a>. This is especially perilous for high-altitude fighter pilots. It is also why flight attendants instruct passengers, in case of loss of cabin pressure, to apply the <a href="/wiki/Oxygen_mask" title="Oxygen mask">oxygen mask</a> to themselves first before helping others; otherwise, one risks losing consciousness.<sup id="cite_ref-solarnav_112-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-solarnav-112">[112]</a></sup></p>
<p>The respiratory centers try to maintain an arterial CO<sub>2</sub> pressure of 40&#160;mm Hg. With intentional hyperventilation, the CO<sub>2</sub> content of arterial blood may be lowered to 10–20&#160;mm Hg (the oxygen content of the blood is little affected), and the respiratory drive is diminished. This is why one can hold one's breath longer after hyperventilating than without hyperventilating. This carries the risk that unconsciousness may result before the need to breathe becomes overwhelming, which is why hyperventilation is particularly dangerous before free diving.</p>
